<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="8547cdfeb4e54e50ad48fc9c8a180d14"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8547cdfeb4e54e50ad48fc9c8a180d14">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'eda5eb11-9c2c-49ea-9cd9-953bc4c9d11c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d104338449" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A novel fence-type superstructure with wide d-spacing in O3-type layered material was proposed. The fence-type superstructure effectively inhibits the formation of vacancy clusters and mitigates the irreversible oxygen loss at a high voltage of 4.4 V. This material with fence-type superstructure displays outstanding cycling performance with a capacity retention of 85.8 % and a voltage retention of 98.5 % after 200 cycles.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee452186-b381-47a8-b60b-847eb1dc8427/anie202404330-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee452186-b381-47a8-b60b-847eb1dc8427/anie202404330-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2d5db44-1b95-4f98-a2b7-843f3dc55f64/anie202404330-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee452186-b381-47a8-b60b-847eb1dc8427/anie202404330-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d104338480" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Enhancing the energy density of layered oxide cathode materials is of great significance for realizing high-performance sodium-ion batteries and promoting their commercial application. Lattice oxygen redox at high voltage usually enables a high capacity and energy density. But the structural degradation, severe voltage decay, and the resultant poor cycling performance caused by irreversible oxygen release seriously restrict the practical application. Herein we introduce a novel fence-type superstructure (2<i>a</i>×3<i>a</i> type supercell) into O3-type layered cathode material Na<sub>0.9</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and achieve a stable cycling performance at a high voltage of 4.4 V. The fence-type superstructure effectively inhibits the formation of the vacancy clusters resulting from out-of-plane Li migration and in-plane transition metal migration at high voltage due to the wide d-spacing, thereby significantly reducing the irreversible release of lattice oxygen and greatly stabilizing the crystal structure. The cathode exhibits a high energy density of 545 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, a high rate capability (112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C) and a high cycling stability (85.8 %@200 cycles with a high initial capacity of 148.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C) accompanied by negligible voltage attenuation (98.5 %@200 cycles). This strategy provides a distinct spacing effect of superstructure to design stable high-voltage layered cathode materials for Na-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202404330-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202404330-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the past decade, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently emerged as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the realm of large-scale energy storage because of the abundance and low cost of sodium resources compared with their lithium counterparts.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0001_R_d104338348e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> The larger ionic radius (1.02 Å for Na<sup>+</sup> and 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>) and higher atomic weight (23 g mol<sup>−1</sup> for Na and 7 g mol<sup>−1</sup> for Li) however lead to a much lower energy density of the cathode for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0004_R_d104338348e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> O3-type layered materials have great commercial prospects due to their high theoretical specific capacity, but their electrochemical properties in practical applications are still far from satisfactory, although various modification strategies including elemental doping, surface coating and electrolyte regulation, have been dedicated.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0007_R_d104338348e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Triggering lattice oxygen redox at high voltage is an effective strategy to realize the high energy density of cathode materials. The lattice oxygen reaction can allow more Na<sup>+</sup> ions to be extracted and inserted, contributing additional capacity compared to traditional transition-metal redox reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0010_R_d104338348e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Constructing a superstructure via substitution/doping of Li element in the layered cathode is a common approach to activate the lattice oxygen redox. The most typical superstructure denoted as honeycomb-type often appears in lithium-rich layered oxide materials<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0014_R_d104338348e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a></sup></span> and P2-type sodium layered oxides,<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0015_R_d104338348e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> which is constructed by the ordered arrangement of Li/TM (transition metal) in TM layers with a √3<i>a</i>×√3<i>a</i> type supercell. Wu et al. reported a P2-layered cathode material with a honeycomb-type superstructure, which delivers a high specific capacity of 266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within a wide voltage range of 1.5–4.6 V, deriving from the oxygen anionic redox induced by the Na−O−Li configuration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0016_R_d104338348e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6a</a></sup></span> A P2-type layered material with a dual honeycomb superstructure reported by Wang et al. also shows a high specific capacity (285.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) in the voltage range of 1.5–4.5 V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0017_R_d104338348e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6b</a></sup></span> Otherwise, to date, cathode materials related to superstructure in SIBs are almost all P2/P3-type structures,<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0015_R_d104338348e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0018_R_d104338348e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> and only a few literature on honeycomb-type superstructure have been reported in O3-type materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0024_R_d104338348e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Another important concern on the superstructure is that only limited cyclic performances of the cathode with the common honeycomb-type superstructure at high voltage have been reported in the literature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0015_R_d104338348e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> This is probably due to the irreversible lattice oxygen loss occurring at high voltage above 4.0 V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0027_R_d104338348e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> The lattice oxygen loss in honeycomb-type superstructure may be caused by the formation of vacancy clusters and molecular O<sub>2</sub> at the charged state.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0030_R_d104338348e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span> To increase the stability of lattice oxygen redox, a novel ribbon-like superstructure in P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> has been proposed recently.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0031_R_d104338348e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nature">11</a></sup></span> Compared with the honeycomb-type superstructure, the ribbon-type superstructure is more stable because it can suppress the vacancy clusters caused by TM migration, and avoid the formation of molecular O<sub>2</sub>. Inspired by this important progress, the development of a new and robust superstructure in the cathode to inhibit the vacancy clusters is a promising strategy to simultaneously obtain high capacity and high stability but remains a sharp challenge for the O3-type layered cathode.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report a unique fence-type superstructure (2<i>a</i>×3<i>a</i> type supercell) with wide superlattice spacing (d=0.51 nm) in O3-type layered Na<sub>0.9</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. This cathode not only delivers a high initial capacity of 167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of 545 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, but also shows a good capacity retention of 85.8 % with a negligible voltage attenuation after 200 cycles at 1 C in the voltage range of 2.0 V–4.4 V, outperforming the cathode with common honeycomb-type superstructure and disordered material without superstructure. The correlation between electrochemical behaviour and local structures is investigated through various in situ and ex situ characterizations. We demonstrate that the proposed fence-type superstructure can effectively suppress the formation of vacancy clusters due to wide superlattice spacing and restrain lattice oxygen loss at high voltage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202404330-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202404330-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Na<sub>0.9</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with fence-type superstructure (denoted as F-NLNMT hereafter) were synthesized by a common solid-state method at temperature of 1000 °C, while the NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.25</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with Li/TM disordered arrangement (D-NLNMT) and with honeycomb-type superstructure (H-NLNMT) were synthesized with the same procedure at lower temperatures of 900 °C and 950 °C, respectively. The elemental composition in the transition metal layers of the as-prepared materials is the same as the designed chemical formula, except that the Na content is slightly lower in the F-NLNMT due to Na volatilization at higher temperatures, confirmed by inductively coupled plasma (ICP) results in Table S1. The superstructure in the F-NLNMT is firstly supported by the powder X-ray diffraction patterns (XRD) and corresponding Rietveld refinement as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1</a>. The refinement details are listed in Table S2. Most Bragg reflections in the XRD pattern of F-NLNMT are well assigned to an O3-type structure with an <i>R-3m</i> space group, similar to the D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT. Importantly, obvious variations in the region of 17–22° are observed in the pattern of F-NLNMT compared to the D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1d</a>–<a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">f</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1d</a>, no peak is observed in the region of 17–22° in the XRD pattern of D-NLNMT, corresponding to the cation-disordered structure in TM layers (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1g</a>). For H-NLNMT, an extra peak at 2θ=20.4° in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1e</a> is displayed and typically suggests a well-known honeycomb-type superstructure (with a √3<i>a</i>×√3<i>a</i> type supercell, <i>a</i> is the distance between two adjacent metal ions in the TM layers, Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1h</a>), derived from Li and TM cation ordering in TM layers, similar to the P2-type materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0019_R_d104338348e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7a - J. Mater. Chem. A">7a</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0021_R_d104338348e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7c - Nat. Chem.">7c</a>-<a href="#anie202404330-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0023_R_d104338348e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7e - Adv. Mater.">7e</a></sup></span> Interestingly, it is noteworthy that a diffraction peak at 2θ=17.2° appears in the pattern of F-NLNMT (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1f</a>), indicating the formation of a superstructure that is noticeably different from the common honeycomb-type structure. According to the Bragg equation, the peaks at 2θ=17.2° in F-NLNMT can be converted to a d-value of 0.51 nm, which may correspond to a 2<i>a</i>×3<i>a</i> type supercell (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0001">1i</a> and Figure S1). This special superstructure shows a wider spacing distance compared to the honeycomb-type superstructure (d=0.43 nm). In order to distinguish it from the common honeycomb-type superstructure, we call it the fence-type superstructure. The strong association between the superstructure peaks and Li/TM cationic ordering in the XRD patterns can be confirmed by neutron powder diffraction (NPD), as shown in Figure S2. The O3 structure of F-NLNMT was confirmed by high angle annular dark field–scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images and the existence of the superstructure was further evidenced by selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (Figure S3).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8a25675d-e3d9-4679-8f42-fd3b0bde9a16/anie202404330-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8a25675d-e3d9-4679-8f42-fd3b0bde9a16/anie202404330-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c8a38248-d348-45c8-b40d-5353556d886e/anie202404330-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8a25675d-e3d9-4679-8f42-fd3b0bde9a16/anie202404330-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202404330-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202404330" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement results for (a) D-NLNMT, (b) H-NLNMT and (c) F-NLNMT. The Rietveld refinement was performed using space group <i>R-3m</i>. Highlighted XRD patterns (17–22°) for (d) D-NLNMT, (e) H-NLNMT and (f) F-NLNMT to distinguish the different local structures. The corresponding structure of (g) D-NLNMT, (h) H-NLNMT and (i) F-NLNMT.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of F-NLNMT is evaluated and compared with the D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT, to understand the influence of the new fence-type superstructure on the electrochemical properties at high voltage. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0002">2a</a>–<a href="#anie202404330-fig-0002">c</a> displays the initial two charge–discharge voltage profiles of the three materials in the voltage range of 2.0–4.4 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at 0.1 C. All three materials deliver higher charge capacities than the theoretical capacity contributed by the redox of transition metal Ni (179 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for D-NLNMT/H-NLNMT, and 158 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for F-NLNMT). The extra capacity implies that anionic lattice oxygen redox reaction is involved in capacity contribution, which is consistent with previous literature that Li substitution can activate the redox activity of oxygen elements at high voltage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0016_R_d104338348e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6a</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0032_R_d104338348e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Joule">12</a></sup></span> Although D-NLNMT delivers a high charge capacity in the first cycle, the initial discharge capacity is only 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to an energy density of 525 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), leading to a poor coulombic efficiency of 72 %. The H-NLNMT delivers a slightly improved discharge capacity of 165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (534 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) with a coulombic efficiency of 76 %. However, the H-NLNMT shows a huge voltage hysteresis in the first cycle, similar to that of D-NLNMT. Such irreversible capacity and voltage hysteresis demonstrate the instability of crystal structure or irreversible oxygen-redox reaction at high voltage even with the presence of a honeycomb-type superstructure. In addition, in the second cycle, the coulombic efficiency of H-NLNMT increases to ~90 %, and the voltage hysteresis is mitigated, which suggests the distinct reaction mechanisms between the initial two charge–discharge processes. This can be more intuitively reflected by the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves, as shown in Figure S4. The oxidation peak at ~3.2 V (marked by “A”) shifts towards higher potential by 156 mV and the oxidation current of the peak at high voltage (marked by “B”) greatly decreases in the second CV curve, indicating an irreversible phase transition caused by structural change at high voltage. Based on the above results, it is reasonable to assume that although H-NLNMT delivers improved electrochemical performance under high voltage, it may still experience the release of O<sub>2</sub> and structural changes during the initial charge–discharge process, which is consistent with typical Li-rich layered oxide materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0014_R_d104338348e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0030_R_d104338348e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span> In sharp contrast, F-NLNMT exhibits a higher initial capacity of 167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (545 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), higher coulombic efficiency (92.5 %) and much smaller voltage hysteresis in the first cycle. Moreover, the peak B at high voltage in the CV curves almost overlaps without noticeable change in the first two cycles, as shown in Figure S5. These excellent performances suggest the fence-type superstructure is more effective in mitigating structural changes than the common honeycomb superstructure under high voltage.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4658dfe8-11f6-4797-a16c-50798271b9c5/anie202404330-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4658dfe8-11f6-4797-a16c-50798271b9c5/anie202404330-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6472af6d-0f05-443c-9f5c-8eb8b3c5a577/anie202404330-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4658dfe8-11f6-4797-a16c-50798271b9c5/anie202404330-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202404330-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202404330" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>First two charge–discharge voltage profiles of (a) D-NLNMT, (b) H-NLNMT and (c) F-NLNMT. (d) Cycling performance and (e) rate capability of D-NLNMT, H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT. (f) Comparison of specific capacity and capacity retention between F-NLNMT and other O3-type high-voltage materials at 1 C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The advantages of the fence-type superstructure were further verified by the durable long-term cyclic stability and good rate capability of F-NLNMT. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0002">2d</a> compares the cycling performance of D-NLNMT, H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT at 1 C. F-NLNMT delivers a remarkable initial discharge capacity of 148.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 85.8 % after 200 cycles, which is much better than that of H-NLNMT (136.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 74.2 % of retention) and D-NLNMT (124.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 60.9 % of retention). Notably, F-NLNMT maintains a high capacity of 127.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, which is even higher than the initial discharge capacity of D-NLNMT at 1 C. Besides, F-NLNMT exhibits minimal voltage attenuation during the long-term cycling, maintaining an average voltage of 98.5 % after 200 cycles, much higher than 92.5 % for H-NLNMT and 89.6 % for D-NLNMT (Figure S6a). Furthermore, F-NLNMT can maintain 84.5 % of the energy density after 200 cycles, while the energy density retention of D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT is only 54.6 % and 68.7 %, respectively (Figure S6b). The rate capacities of F-NLNMT, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0002">2e</a>, are also higher than those of D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT at various current densities. The F-NLNMT shows a remarkable capacity of 112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C, corresponding to 67.3 % of the capacity at 0.1 C. In addition, when the current density turns back to 0.1 C, the F-NLNMT can recover its specific capacity. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was applied to prove the high-rate capability of F-NLNMT. F-NLNMT exhibits higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients than D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT during the charge and discharge process (Figure S7). Specifically, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of D-NLNMT and H-NLNMT show a sudden decrease above 4.0 V, which is caused by the out-of-plane migration of TM, while the same phenomenon was not observed in F-NLNMT. The gradual decrease of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients above 4.2 V is attributed to the P3-OP2 phase transition and sluggish kinetics of oxygen redox. By evaluating the electrochemical performances operating at high voltage, F-NLNMT outperforms most previously reported O3-type cathode materials in terms of reversible capacity, cycling stability and rate capability (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0002">2f</a> and Table S3).</p>
         
         <p>According to the above electrochemical tests, the superstructure is effective in improving the cycling stability of the material compared to the Li/TM disordered structure and the fence-type superstructure is more capable than the common honeycomb-type superstructure. Since the morphologies (Figure S8) and chemical valence of transition metals (Figure S9) among the F-NLNMT and H-NLNMT are almost identical, the electrochemical performances are hence possibly related to the different configurations of the superstructure. To deeply understand the mechanism of fence-type superstructure for the structural evolution at high voltage, we conducted in situ XRD experiments on the first cycle of both F-NLNMT and H-NLNMT at 0.1 C. The original in situ XRD patterns are presented in Figure S10 and S11. It should be mentioned that for the in situ XRD patterns of both F-NLNMT and H-NLNMT, a significant increase in the relative intensity of (003) and (006) peaks is observed compared with the powder XRD patterns. This phenomenon results from the preferential orientation of cathode materials during the electrode preparation process, which is especially noticeable for layered oxide materials in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0033_R_d104338348e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Mater.">13</a></sup></span> For H-NLNMT (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0003">3a</a>), at the beginning of the charge process, the (003) diffraction peak gradually shifts to a lower angle, indicating the expansion of the <i>c</i> lattice parameter caused by increased O<sup>2−</sup>−O<sup>2−</sup> repulsive force between adjacent oxygen planes. The (003) peak then splits into two peaks, suggesting the occurrence of phase transition. According to previous works, the newly formed phase can be indexed to a distorted O’3 phase derived from the Jahn–Teller distortion of Ni<sup>3+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0034_R_d104338348e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span> Upon further charge, the O’3 phase gradually transforms into the P3 phase inferred by the diminishment of the (104) peak and the appearance of (015) peak at 2θ=45.2°, accompanied by the gliding of the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. After complete transformation, the P3 phase is maintained, and the (003)<sub>P3</sub> peak monotonically shifts towards lower angles until charged to 4.0 V, indicating a single-phase evolution in this voltage range.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0034_R_d104338348e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span> Starting at 4.0 V, a dramatic peak shift to higher angles and peak broadening can be observed for (003)<sub>P3</sub> peak, characteristic of the so-called OP2-phase, in which the prismatic and octahedral NaO<sub>6</sub> layers are stacked alternately along the <i>c</i>-axis direction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0035_R_d104338348e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> Compared with the common direct P3-O1 or P3-O3’ phase transition in the O3-type materials, although the P3-OP2 phase transition is believed to reduce the damage to the structure,<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0038_R_d104338348e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span> the H-NLNMT still undergoes a substantial structural change at high voltage. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0003">3c</a> illustrates the evolution of the <i>c</i> lattice parameter of H-NLNMT calculated from XRD data. It can be seen that once the OP2 phase appears above 4.0 V, the <i>c</i> lattice parameter contracts considerably, and the maximum change in the <i>c</i> lattice parameter during the OP2 phase region is as high as 1.28 Å (8.24 %). Such a large contraction would adversely affect the structure stability, leading to an increase in lattice strain and further particle cracking. In the reverse discharge process, the XRD patterns exhibit an opposite evolution manner. The OP2 phase transforms back into the P3 phase and finally returns to the original O3 phase.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/05febbc4-b0b1-40f9-8ff2-c8207f538473/anie202404330-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/05febbc4-b0b1-40f9-8ff2-c8207f538473/anie202404330-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a517a57e-cedc-4a04-9928-f7d9f4af40ff/anie202404330-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/05febbc4-b0b1-40f9-8ff2-c8207f538473/anie202404330-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202404330-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202404330" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>In situ X-ray diffraction patterns of (a) H-NLNMT and (b) F-NLNMT over the first cycle at a current rate of 0.1 C between 2.0–4.4 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. (c, d) Corresponding evolution of the c lattice parameter.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>F-NLNMT exhibits a similar phase transition behaviour during the first cycle, but the evolutions of the diffraction peak and <i>c</i> lattice parameter become more gradual and continuous at low voltage regions (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0003">3b</a> and <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0003">3d</a>). The F-NLNMT also undergoes a similar P3-OP2 phase transition above 4.0 V like H-NLNMT. But importantly, the maximum change in the <i>c</i> lattice parameter of F-NLNMT at high voltage is only 0.32 Å (1.93 %), which is much smaller than that in H-NLNMT. Figure S12 compares the evolution of <i>a</i> and <i>V</i> lattice parameters of H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT. Both <i>a</i> and <i>V</i> lattice parameters in F-NLNMT show smaller changes, especially the change of <i>V</i> is much smaller than that of H-NLNMT (10.58 % for H-NLNMT and 3.59 % for F-NLNMT). The in situ XRD experiment was also conducted on D-NLNMT, as shown in Figure S13. In the absence of superstructure, D-NLNMT exhibits an asymmetric phase transition and more drastic structural changes than those of H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT. The in situ XRD analysis suggests that the fence-type superstructure is effective in the suppression of structural damage during the P3-OP2 phase transition at high voltage, which may account for enhanced electrochemical performances.</p>
         
         <p>Raman spectroscopy was further employed to directly probe the structural change during cycling as it is very sensitive to the local structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0039_R_d104338348e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> The Raman spectra of H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT were recorded at different charge and discharge states, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">d</a>. For pristine H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT, the intense peaks at ~596 and ~500 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be assigned to symmetric TM−O bond stretching (A<sub>1g</sub>) and asymmetric Na−O bond stretching (E<sub>2g</sub>), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0041_R_d104338348e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17b - Nat. Mater.">17b</a></sup></span> During charging from pristine to 4.0 V, the intensity of E<sub>2g</sub> peaks in both materials decreases with decreasing Na content. Meanwhile, the A<sub>1g</sub> peaks shift to a higher wavenumber due to the shortening of the TM−O bond as a result of transition metal oxidation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0040_R_d104338348e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17a - Phys. Rev. B">17a</a></sup></span> Notably, for H-NLNMT, a new peak at 680 cm<sup>−1</sup> (denoted as A’<sub>1g</sub>) appears at 4.4 V and disappears when discharged to 2.0 V (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4c</a>). According to previous reports, this new peak is characteristic of a distorted TM−O bond attributed to out-of-plane cation migration to Na layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0041_R_d104338348e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17b - Nat. Mater.">17b</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0042_R_d104338348e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem.">18</a></sup></span> This result confirms the local structure is damaged in H-NLNMT at high voltage. In sharp contrast, no appearance of the A′<sub>1g</sub> peak is observed for F-NLNMT when charged to 4.4 V (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4d</a>), further corroborating the robust structure of F-NLNMT stabilized by the fence-type superstructure.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/218ebe67-1f74-49c0-8cdf-77ea1ea89934/anie202404330-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/218ebe67-1f74-49c0-8cdf-77ea1ea89934/anie202404330-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4a072520-59ed-4e51-9c32-64a5bd32afdf/anie202404330-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/218ebe67-1f74-49c0-8cdf-77ea1ea89934/anie202404330-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202404330-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202404330" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Ex situ Raman spectra of H-NLNMT (a, c) and F-NLNMT (b, d) electrodes at different states. Ex situ Ni K-edge XANES spectra of (e) H-NLNMT and (f) F-NLNMT electrodes at different states. Ex situ Mn K-edge XANES spectra of (g) H-NLNMT and (h) F-NLNMT electrodes at different states.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ex situ hard X-ray absorption spectroscopy (h-XAS) was also applied to investigate the valence state variations of Ni, Mn and Ti in the F-NLNMT and H-NLNMT during cycling. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4e</a> shows the Ni K-edge X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectra of H-NLNMT at different states. When charged to 4.0 V, a conspicuous absorption edge shift to higher energy (~3 eV) can be observed, suggesting that Ni underwent an oxidation process from Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>4+</sup>. Upon further charging to 4.2 V, the Ni K-edge barely shifts compared with that at 4.0 V. This minor shift of Ni K-edge implies that oxygen-redox is activated above 4.0 V and mainly contributes to the capacity at high voltage. Unexpectedly, the Ni K-edge shows an aberrant shift towards lower energy when further charging to 4.4 V, indicative of the reduced valence state of Ni. This abnormal phenomenon of TM reduction may be caused by lattice oxygen loss, similar to the reports of Li-rich cathodes and high-Ni layered cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0043_R_d104338348e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> Note that the P3-OP2 phase transition also occurs at ~4.0 V in both materials, as determined from the in situ XRD patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0003">3</a>). This similarity is not fortuitous and implies that the structural change may be closely related to the lattice oxygen redox. After discharging to 2.0 V, the Ni K-edge shifts back to its pristine state, indicating the good reversibility of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox. In addition to the reduction of Ni, the Mn K-edge spectra in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4g</a> also reflect local structural changes at high voltage. The intensity of Mn pre-edge peaks increases at 4.4 V, suggesting the distortion of MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which may be also induced by lattice oxygen loss. The XAS analysis of H-NLNMT manifests that the irreversible lattice oxygen redox may mainly contribute to the structural instability and low columbic efficiency of H-NLNMT with honeycomb-type superstructure. In contrast, the Ni K-edge of F-NLNMT at 4.4 V shows a neglectable shift to lower energy compared to that at 4.0 V (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4f</a>), implying that the reduction of Ni is mitigated in F-NLNMT. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4h</a> shows that the Mn K-edge spectra at charged states almost coincide with negligible change in the intensity of pre-edge peaks. In addition, the Ti K-edge spectra can also confirm the difference in structural stability between H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT, as shown in Figure S14. These results demonstrate that the lattice oxygen loss has been prohibited in the material with a fence-type superstructure.</p>
         
         <p>To directly monitor the lattice oxygen loss, in situ differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy (DEMS) experiments were performed during the first two cycles. The signal of O<sub>2</sub> (m/z=32) and CO<sub>2</sub> (m/z=44) was monitored simultaneously. However, no direct O<sub>2</sub> release (Figure S15), but a considerable amount of CO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5b</a>) was detected in our experiments. According to previous reports, the highly active oxygen species on the surface or molecule O<sub>2</sub> released from the lattice oxygen of the oxide cathode react with the carbonate electrolyte to generate CO<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0048_R_d104338348e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> Therefore, the signal of CO<sub>2</sub> in the DEMS, to a large extent, is related to the release of oxygen. Overall, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5b</a>, F-NLNMT demonstrates a considerably diminished amount of CO<sub>2</sub> compared with H-NLNMT under identical experimental conditions, suggesting again the lattice oxygen loss is suppressed with the fence-type superstructure, in line with the XAS results. In detail, we could divide four-steps of CO<sub>2</sub> evolution during the first charge of H-NLNMT as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5a</a>. There is no CO<sub>2</sub> in step 1 as transition metal cations are predominantly oxidized in this region. The CO<sub>2</sub> is generated above ~3.3 V, which may be ascribed to the electrochemical decomposition of residual Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of H-NLNMT determined by the XPS analysis (Figure S16 and Table S4, step 2), consistent with the previously reported decomposition voltage of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0052_R_d104338348e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> An abrupt increase in CO<sub>2</sub> evolution is then observed above ~4.0 V (step 3), confirming the beginning of the lattice oxygen redox reaction, which is consistent with the XAS results that the Ni K-edge barely shifts when above 4.0 V and again implies the structural change is closely related to the lattice oxygen redox. Starting from 4.0 V, the evolution rate of CO<sub>2</sub> increases continuously with voltage, reaches a maximum at 4.2 V, and then slowly decreases until 4.4 V (step 4). This CO<sub>2</sub> release trend can be interpreted that, as the voltage increases, more lattice oxygen participates in the redox reaction, and the lattice oxygen becomes more reactive, increasing the evolution rate of CO<sub>2</sub> in step 3. With the continuous loss of lattice oxygen, the surface of the H-NLNMT becomes oxygen-depleted. The passivated surface layer prevents the further decomposition reaction of the lattice oxygen and carbonate electrolyte, resulting in a decrease in the CO<sub>2</sub> evolution rate in step 4. In addition, a notable CO<sub>2</sub> release peak is observed at the end of the first discharge in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5a</a>, probably caused by the decomposition of the carbonate electrolyte due to the dissolution of Mn<sup>2+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0055_R_d104338348e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> As depicted in CV curves (Figure S4), the Mn<sup>4+</sup> in H-NLNMT is partly reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup>, as confirmed by the reduction current in the low voltage region (marked by “C”). Then the disproportionation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> (2Mn<sup>3+</sup>↔Mn<sup>2+</sup>+Mn<sup>4+</sup>) leads to the dissolution of Mn<sup>2+</sup> which in turn results in the decomposition of the electrolyte at low voltage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0055_R_d104338348e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0058_R_d104338348e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a></sup></span> The same CO<sub>2</sub> peak repeatedly occurs at the end of the second discharge, which is consistent with the CV curves in Figure S4. Since H-NLNMT almost depleted the surface lattice oxygen during the first cycle, the CO<sub>2</sub> is significantly reduced in the second charge process. However, a considerable amount of CO<sub>2</sub> was still detected, indicating continuous oxygen release during the subsequent charge/discharge cycles. The successive release of lattice oxygen from the host structure and the participation of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction results in the fast decay of the average voltage of H-NLNMT during long-term cycling (Figure S6). In the case of F-NLNMT, a different CO<sub>2</sub> release behaviour has been observed. There is also no CO<sub>2</sub> release in step 1, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5b</a>. In step 2, F-NLNMT shows less CO<sub>2</sub> release than H-NLNMT due to less Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface, as confirmed by XPS results (Figure S17 and Table S5). In step 3 starting from ~4.0 V, the CO<sub>2</sub> evolution rate first increases but remains constant with time/voltage until 4.4 V. This difference confirms that compared with H-NLNMT, the lattice oxygen redox in F-NLNMT is highly stable and the release of oxygen is greatly suppressed. In addition, the absence of a CO<sub>2</sub> release peak at the end of the discharge indicates that Mn<sup>4+</sup> was not reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and served to stabilize the host structure of F-NLNMT.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/80f4dfa9-1503-470c-a285-e15b2aee0d6d/anie202404330-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/80f4dfa9-1503-470c-a285-e15b2aee0d6d/anie202404330-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1069d490-d1d1-46d4-a1cf-f3782cefa496/anie202404330-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/80f4dfa9-1503-470c-a285-e15b2aee0d6d/anie202404330-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202404330-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202404330" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>DEMS results for (a) H-NLNMT and (b) F-NLNMT for the first 2 cycles. RIXS spectra at an excitation energy of 531 eV for (c) H-NLNMT and (e) F-NLNMT at different states. (d) Low-energy loss feature for H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT at charged state (4.4 V).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The resonant inelastic X-ray scattering (RIXS) technique was further employed to verify the good stability of lattice oxygen in the F-NLNMT since it can determine the states of O in the bulk.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0059_R_d104338348e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Proc. Nat. Acad. Sci.">24</a></sup></span> The ex situ O K-edge RIXS spectra for H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT electrodes at various states are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5c</a>–<a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">e</a>. For H-NLNMT, a broad peak at ~7.6 eV (labelled as A) and a progression of sharp peaks between 0 and 2 eV are observed at the charged state in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5c</a>. Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5d</a> shows that the interval between the first two peaks is 0.189 eV, equivalent to 1525 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is a typical feature of molecular O<sub>2</sub> as reported in the literature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202404330-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0030_R_d104338348e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0031_R_d104338348e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nature">11</a>, <a href="#anie202404330-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202404330-bib-0059_R_d104338348e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Proc. Nat. Acad. Sci.">24</a></sup></span> These results can provide evidence that the lattice oxygen in H-NLNMT was lost at high voltage in the form of molecular O<sub>2</sub> release, which is consistent with the results of DEMS. In comparison, the RIXS spectra in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0005">5e</a> show no evidence for the presence of molecular O<sub>2</sub> at the charged state of F-NLNMT, only a weak peak A can be seen, revealing the reversible and stable oxidation of lattice oxygen in F-NLNMT.</p>
         
         <p>Combining the electrochemical behaviours with the in situ and ex situ experimental characterizations, the fence-type superstructure is effective in mitigating lattice oxygen loss at high voltage and the possible explanation is described as follows. During charge, Li ions migrate from the TM layers to the Na layers, leaving corresponding vacancies in the TM layers. At the same time, TM migration in the TM layers is inevitable and facile. In the D-NLNMT, several connected vacancies could be easily formed after the removal of Li, as shown by the red arrows in Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0006">6a</a>, which are native vacancy clusters. In this arrangement, on oxidation of O<sup>2−</sup>, the unstable O−Mn<sub>0</sub> (O<sup>2−</sup> is coordinated by 0 Mn in the TM layer) is stabilized through dimerization with O−Mn<sub>1</sub> to form Mn−O<sub>2</sub> moieties, denoted as trapped molecular O<sub>2</sub>. The molecular O<sub>2</sub> in the bulk of materials can be trapped in vacancy clusters, while the O<sub>2</sub> formed near the surface will be released. Although the formation and reduction of molecular O<sub>2</sub> in the bulk is reversible, the release of O<sub>2</sub> on the surface is not. The irreversible structural change caused by oxygen loss leads to inferior electrochemical performance, the most significant of which is the low initial coulombic efficiency of D-NLNMT. For H-NLNMT, the initial coulombic efficiency and cycling performance are improved compared with D-NLNMT due to the disappearance of native vacancy clusters. However, the d-spacing of superlattice in a honeycomb-type superstructure is only 0.43 nm, which could not effectively inhibit the migration of Mn atoms into adjacent vacancies to generate a vacancy cluster, capable of accommodating molecular O<sub>2</sub>. In addition to in-plane migration, as we can see from Raman spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0004">4c</a>), out-of-plane TM migration to Na layers will also occur to maintain the host structure at high voltage (with low Na content). The easily formed vacancy clusters and subsequent oxygen loss still negatively impact the cycling performance of H-NLNMT. In the case of charged F-NLNMT with wide superlattice spacing (0.51 nm), the vacancy clusters are difficult to form due to the more dispersed vacancies in this structure (Figure <a href="#anie202404330-fig-0006">6c</a>). We further employ DFT to compare the energy differences between the structure with isolated vacancies and vacancy cluster for H-NLNMT and F-NLNMT. As shown in Figure S18, the energy difference from isolated vacancies to a vacancy cluster in fence-type superstructure is higher than that of honeycomb-type superstructure, which confirms that vacancy clusters are more difficult to form in F-NLNMT. The delocalized electron holes on O<sup>2−</sup> and host structure can be stabilized at high voltage, as confirmed by multiple characterization analyses, and hence F-NLNMT could exhibit excellent electrochemical performance. When paired with hard carbon (HC) anode, the F-NLNMT||HC full cell delivers an initial capacity of 145.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and a high capacity of 139.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C (based on the mass of cathode material). Furthermore, the full cell maintains 87.3 % of the initial capacity after 100 cycles at 1 C (Figure S19), demonstrating its potential in practical applications.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202404330-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d1a94b3-1056-4b56-8f1f-28201eb76ba3/anie202404330-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d1a94b3-1056-4b56-8f1f-28201eb76ba3/anie202404330-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/768337af-79b6-4790-b6c5-3a7fd9d8dee9/anie202404330-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d1a94b3-1056-4b56-8f1f-28201eb76ba3/anie202404330-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202404330-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202404330-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we report a unique fence-type superstructure of 2<i>a</i> ×3<i>a</i> type with a wide spacing distance (0.51 nm) in the O3-type layered Na<sub>0.9</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (F-NLNMT) and realize the high energy density and high stability of sodium ion cathode at high voltage (4.4 V). The irreversible lattice oxygen loss at high voltage has been mitigated in F-NLNMT due to a larger spacing distance, compared to the common honeycomb-type superstructure and Li/TM disordered O3-type layered oxides. As a consequence, F-NLNMT exhibits an excellent electrochemical performance at a high cutoff voltage of 4.4 V, in terms of a high reversible capacity of 167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to an energy density of 545 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and an impressive capacity retention of 85.8 % after 200 cycles with negligible voltage attenuation. This work provides a new direction to design the stable superstructure for high-performance cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202404330-sec-0004-title"> Acknowledgments</h2>
